                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8fe7d196f50946f0b58628ac507fbbe7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b322e61f-8d7d-4f1f-a8da-27a2a6ff7c72'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d53838698" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are receiving a great deal of attention as potential sustainable replacements for Li-ion batteries in electric vehicles and grid storage applications. To date, commercialized SIBs offer inferior energy density with passable extended cycling. By contrast, next-generation SIBs will likely utilize layered oxide cathodes that offer improved energy density but to date show inferior stability both during cycling and in terms of stability of the cathodes in air during cell assembly. These properties are highly tunable with composition and herein the promising P2 phases are systematically explored in the Na–Fe–Mn–O phase diagram by making 256 different compositions. The optimal material is a P2 material saturated with Ni (a modest 16% of the transition metal layer) and shows a highly competitive energy density of 640&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> while minimizing the amount of sacrificial sodium needed in full cells and also improving the air stability of the material. This study shows the vital role that thorough systematic screening will play in the continued development of these vital materials for sustainable secondary battery production and provides guidance toward sustainable Na-ion cathodes by minimizing the nickel content required for high performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202400296-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIB) are considered the most promising short-term alternative to lithium-ion batteries. The similarity in the working mechanism of Na-ion and Li-ion batteries, coupled with the abundance of sodium resources, enhances the economic feasibility of wide-scale use of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0001_R_d53838689e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0003_R_d53838689e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the various components in sodium-ion batteries, cathode materials play a pivotal role as they typically limit key metrics related to the battery performance such as the gravimetric capacity (amount of sodium transferred during battery operation). Among a wide range of cathode materials proposed up to now, layered oxide cathode materials of the form Na<sub>x</sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (where M represents one or a mixture of transition metals), have attracted a great deal of attention due to their high energy density, ease of synthesis and adjustable composition including mixtures with only Earth abundant elements like Mn and Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0004_R_d53838689e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0006_R_d53838689e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Physica B+C.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Binary or ternary transition metal systems often show better battery performance than materials with only a single transition metal, though the specific ratios of each element can dramatically impact performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0007_R_d53838689e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Optimizing the chemical composition of layered oxides is therefore of great importance as it controls structural stability, electrochemical performance, air stability, industrial feasibility, and the environmental footprint of the cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0008_R_d53838689e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a>, <a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0009_R_d53838689e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Science.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In particular, Na–Mn–Fe–O layered oxide cathodes have gained significant research interest due to their high energy density, low cost, and environmentally benign elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0010_R_d53838689e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0011_R_d53838689e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, these materials are still suffering from several issues, including poor air stability<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0012_R_d53838689e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and inadequate long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0013_R_d53838689e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They also often tend to have a larger discharge capacity than charge (i.e., more Na can be intercalated into them than was removed during charge), and such negative capacities result in engineering challenges with needing sacrificial sodium in the cell. Previously, we applied high-throughput methods that were developed in our group<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0014_R_d53838689e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Eng. Au.">14</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0016_R_d53838689e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Can. J. Chem.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to study the entire Na–Fe–Mn–O pseudo-ternary system systematically.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0007_R_d53838689e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As summarized in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0001">1a</a> our findings identified seven solid solution regions, including three layered oxide phases of interest for Na-ion cathodes: P2, P3, and O3. Interestingly, both electrochemical performance and air stability are highly tunable within these regions by changing the content of Fe and Mn. For instance, the best air stability was observed in materials with a low Fe content where stability against both carbon dioxide and water was improved. The material P2-Na<sub>0.66</sub>Fe<sub>0.13</sub>Mn<sub>0.87</sub>O<sub>2</sub> had relatively high capacity and improved air stability within the Na–Fe–Mn–O system. However, the improved air stability is still insufficient, and the large negative capacities mean that low capacities are currently obtained in full cells. Thus, far more exploration is still needed to further improve the air stability and electrochemical performance of layered oxide materials relying on Fe and Mn. In another recent study in our group,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0017_R_d53838689e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we systematically investigated the effect of 52 dopants on the Na<sub>0.66</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>M<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2+δ</sub> (M = dopant) material. It was observed that different dopants can change properties dramatically. For instance, certain dopants (e.g., Li) highly improved the stability in the air. Ni-doped material showed one of the highest specific capacities, close to 200&nbsp;mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, but it was not adequately competitive in the air stability experiment among other dopants.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/273ae4c2-f5e9-4c7f-bb70-580ff34d0fac/adsu202400296-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/273ae4c2-f5e9-4c7f-bb70-580ff34d0fac/adsu202400296-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/30c5a546-3115-40f9-b91c-f6d355ffe388/adsu202400296-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/273ae4c2-f5e9-4c7f-bb70-580ff34d0fac/adsu202400296-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The phase stabilities in the entire Na–Fe–Mn–O phase diagram. A secondary axis in turquoise is provided to indicate the Na content as x in Na<sub>x</sub>MO<sub>2</sub>. b) A map of sodium loss during synthesis at different Ni levels in the Na–Mn–Ni–O system. c–f) All four Gibbs’ triangles synthesized for this study with dots showing compositions in each phase stability diagram at four different Na levels (x = 0.55, 0.67, 0.8, and 1). For example, points in c) had nominal compositions Na<sub>0.55</sub>Mn<sub>1-y-z</sub>Fe<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The red dots indicate single-phase P2 materials while the blue show single-phase O3, a single green point in f shows a pure P3 material.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Our two recent studies were quite comprehensive in showing the high potential benefit of tuning materials within key phase stability diagrams (e.g. the pseudo-ternary Na–Fe–Mn–O Gibbs triangle). Given the benefits of Ni seen in our wide substitution study, and the benefits of systematically screening compositions within a system, herein, we chose a further metal substitution approach to thoroughly screen the Na–Fe–Mn–Ni–O system to further improve the properties of the Mn–Fe-based sodium layered oxide cathodes. There are only a limited number of Ni-doped Na–Fe–Mn–O compositions reported to date. Herein, we used high-throughput methods developed in refs. [<span><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0015_R_d53838689e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Comb. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0016_R_d53838689e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Can. J. Chem.">16</a>, <a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0018_R_d53838689e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">18</a></span>] to systematically screen the impact of Ni substitution in the Na–Fe–Mn–O system. Overall, we studied four Gibbs’ triangles with Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>x</sub>NiO<sub>2,</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>FeO<sub>2</sub> as the corners in each triangle, with each triangle having a different x value (sodium contents), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0001">1c–f</a>. Analysis of the 256 samples yields an unquestionable optimum Ni content for improved performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202400296-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adsu202400296-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>First, it is essential to ensure that sodium loss is mitigated during synthesis. It is well known that high Ni contents lead to very high Li loss in Li-layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0019_R_d53838689e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0021_R_d53838689e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Solid State Ionics.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0001">1b</a> shows that Na loss is indeed very high at high Ni contents and well controlled within the blue-shaded region which implies that the nickel content on the transition metal layer must remain below ≈50% for us to make reliable combinatorial samples in the Ni containing materials.</p>
            
            <p>To study the phase distribution of Na–Mn–Fe–Ni–O pseudo quaternary systems, 256 cathode materials were synthesized and surveyed by powder XRD. The points in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0001">1c–f</a> show compositions over the phase diagrams that were synthesized for this study at four different Na levels (<i>x</i> = 0.55, 0.67, 0.8, and 1). All the XRD patterns were visually compared to the reference patterns to identify the phases present as illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0002">2</a> for representative XRD patterns. For Na equals 0.55, 0.67, and 0.8, the only single-phase materials found took the P2 phase, this region is highlighted in red in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0001">1c–f</a>. For Na contents of <i>x =</i> 1, a small single P2 phase region was observed, and a few single P3 (green) and a small single O3 phase (blue) were observed. However, it is worth mentioning that this region (x = 1) is highly impacted by sodium loss as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0001">1</a> such that follow-up work is currently underway preparing pure O3 materials in this region under conditions that minimize Na loss. Multi-phase materials were observed for the rest of the samples in the Gibbs' triangles. To better understand the impact of the Ni substitution in the Na-Mn–Fe–Ni–O pseudo quaternary systems, XRD patterns of single-phase layered materials were fit using Pawley refinement (fits are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0002">2</a>). The resulting lattice parameters are mapped in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0003">3</a>. The <i>a</i> lattice parameter generally decreases as the manganese content increases as expected given the smaller ionic radius of Mn<sup>4+</sup> (0.53 Å) compared to both Fe<sup>3+</sup> (0.645 Å) and Ni<sup>2+</sup> (0.69 Å). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0003">3d–f</a> demonstrates the <i>c</i> parameter generally decreases by increasing the amount of Ni. The fact that the <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> contour plots run nearly perpendicular to each other has been seen previously both in Li<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0022_R_d53838689e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0007_R_d53838689e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> layered oxides, the data here follows this trend showing that substitutions that enlarge the spacing within the layers typically enable tighter packing of those layers.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb381d8e-5b89-4dff-9b7e-e731771d352a/adsu202400296-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb381d8e-5b89-4dff-9b7e-e731771d352a/adsu202400296-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a0c38452-074d-490e-91c7-14f0b4cf6a39/adsu202400296-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb381d8e-5b89-4dff-9b7e-e731771d352a/adsu202400296-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8fc4ac78-d898-4ef4-b7ee-31c952f3a5a3/adsu202400296-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8fc4ac78-d898-4ef4-b7ee-31c952f3a5a3/adsu202400296-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5b9eb6d4-efc2-4f31-bfd6-ae86742c4fda/adsu202400296-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8fc4ac78-d898-4ef4-b7ee-31c952f3a5a3/adsu202400296-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adsu202400296-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the impact of Ni substitution into Na–Fe–Mn–O cathodes, high-throughput cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed on all compositions within the 4 phase stability diagrams at different Na levels. CVs for zoom-in <i>x</i> = 0.67 are plotted in a Gibbs’ triangle in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0004">4</a> and the corresponding voltage curves are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0005">5</a>. These graphs show that the electrochemical profiles evolve dramatically with compositions, especially within the single-phase regions. Three main redox peaks are observed (1) peaks at 2.1 – 2.4&nbsp;V (commonly attributed to Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>), (2) peaks at 3.2 – 4.0&nbsp;V (may be attributed to Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup>), and (3) peaks near 3.2 – 3.6&nbsp;V (common for Ni<sup>2+/3+/4+</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0023_R_d53838689e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">23</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0025_R_d53838689e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These peak attributions are well-established in the literature and re-considered in the XAS/XPS section below. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0004">4</a> demonstrates that the redox activity in Na–Fe–Mn–O cathodes is highly dependent on the composition. For instance, for Mn-rich samples, the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox peaks are seen predominantly, while the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/<sup>4+</sup> redox peaks grow with more Fe and Ni contents, respectively. Overall, CV curves are smoother for single P2 phase materials which are well known to correspond to a suppressed phase transition during electrochemical cycling and thereby lead to better extended cycling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e5964183-571c-4e66-aaa5-a8e56e2bf2c5/adsu202400296-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e5964183-571c-4e66-aaa5-a8e56e2bf2c5/adsu202400296-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03a27774-408b-4c1a-b4f8-9f1084aa00fa/adsu202400296-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e5964183-571c-4e66-aaa5-a8e56e2bf2c5/adsu202400296-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The first cycle of the combinatorial cell made for Na = 0.67. The pure P2 phase samples are highlighted in light blue. The cyclic voltammetry is performed between 1.5 and 4.3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;V h<sup>-1</sup>. Compositions with the highest capacities are shown in red rectangles and discussed extensively in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0007">7</a>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/21d380c8-fb02-4192-aa8d-f0d7eea0c2cd/adsu202400296-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/21d380c8-fb02-4192-aa8d-f0d7eea0c2cd/adsu202400296-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3404fe79-4e4b-4b38-b6e0-deb23623159a/adsu202400296-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/21d380c8-fb02-4192-aa8d-f0d7eea0c2cd/adsu202400296-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The first 1.5 cycles of the combinatorial cell made for Na = 0.67. The pure P2 phase samples are highlighted in light blue. Voltage curves for compositions with the highest capacities are shown in the red rectangles and shown in greater detail in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0007">7</a>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0005">5</a> shows the voltage curves for the same zoom-in <i>x</i> = 0.67 compositions for the first one-and-a-half cycles. Voltage curves reveal important trends that are not apparent in CVs, such as overpotential (a vertical offset between the charge and the discharge plateaus in the voltage curves). Some important trends can be seen in the voltage curves including the systematic increase in overpotential seen as the Fe content increases (this is a sharp change as we go from the 3<sup>rd</sup> to 4<sup>th</sup> row from the bottom in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0005">5</a>), and the overall decrease in negative capacities as nickel content increases (more on this below, but this is easily seen in the two voltage curves shown in the red triangles). The clear evolution of cycling performance will be further explored by extracting key battery metrics from these CVs.</p>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties for all sodium levels are extracted and summarized in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0006">6</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a> (Supporting Information). The first cycle discharge capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0006">6a–d</a>) shows a clear hot spot with an optimum composition in the <i>x</i> = 0.67 diagram. The hot spot corresponds to the highest nickel content pure P2 sample. In other words, the key to maximizing the capacity of the Ni-doped samples is to saturate the P2 phase with Ni. As we found previously in the Na–Fe–Mn–O system, the highest capacities are not obtained with maximal sodium content, but in fact, <i>x =</i> 0.6 to 0.67 seems optimal, this is again the case here. The amount of overpotential across the phase diagrams is plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) and again generally shows the benefit of increasing nickel, as do the plots of average discharge voltage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0006">6e–h</a>). These findings are consistent with the higher voltage of the nickel redox couples, particularly in comparison to manganese. Moreover, the negative capacity also shows a strong suppression with increasing nickel content as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0006">6i–l</a>. Here, negative capacity is defined as the difference between the first charge and the first discharge. A high value for negative capacity means that a large amount of sacrificial sodium is needed in a full cell, a cell engineering challenge that has not yet been met. As such, a low (or preferably zero) negative capacity is needed for immediate use in commercial cells. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0006">6i–l</a>, negative capacity decreases by increasing the amount of Ni in all four pseudo-quaternary systems. We therefore clearly identify the optimal material from a battery performance standpoint as the Ni-saturated P2 phase with a sodium content of 0.67.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b721e2f-01c4-43df-8082-712e6b52098d/adsu202400296-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b721e2f-01c4-43df-8082-712e6b52098d/adsu202400296-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7144213b-eff2-40e0-8ded-213ff210e955/adsu202400296-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b721e2f-01c4-43df-8082-712e6b52098d/adsu202400296-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties for all compositions at sodium contents x = 0.55, 0.67, 0.8, and 1 extracted from the combinatorial Cells. Evolution of different battery properties across the phase stability diagrams: a–d) 1<sup>st</sup> discharge specific capacities, e–h) first discharge average voltage, and i–l) negative capacity (1<sup>st</sup> charge capacity minus the 1<sup>st</sup> discharge capacity).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adsu202400296-sec-0050-title">2.3 The Optimum Composition</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0007">7</a> shows the electrochemical performance of the two optimal materials identified in the high-throughput screening (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0004">4</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0006">6</a>). In particular, the best material with x = 0.67 (Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) shows a discharge energy of 640&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> over the full voltage range (1.5–4.3&nbsp;V) with a high capacity of 217 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and an average voltage of 2.95&nbsp;V. Although highly impressive, it is important to note that in full cells with hard carbon anodes, a low voltage capacity below 2&nbsp;V is not practical. We therefore also calculated the energy density in the range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The best x = 0.67 material now has a capacity of 164 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, an average voltage of 3.32&nbsp;V, and an energy density of 545&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. With these integration limits, the negative capacity is negligible such that sacrificial sodium is not required. These values are superior to those of the best O3 Na–Mn–Ni–Fe–O materials which achieve capacities of ∼145 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a comparable average voltage to our P2 materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0026_R_d53838689e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This shows that the common assumptions about O3 being the ideal phase to avoid the need for sacrificial sodium need to be revisited, the properties of the P2 phase are highly composition dependent and the P2 materials with optimal composition identified here show very competitive performance in comparison to O3 materials, and are also generally far more stable in air, as will be explored further below. It is also important to recognize that the optimal P2 material developed herein shows good performance up to 4.3&nbsp;V without irreversible capacity at high voltage. This is not always the case in other Na-ion cathodes and will be the subject of future study into these materials.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c5778a1b-fd7c-4eca-bb21-1cc22c72a027/adsu202400296-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c5778a1b-fd7c-4eca-bb21-1cc22c72a027/adsu202400296-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dbdc9973-06e3-4b6e-9672-ad049b8127f6/adsu202400296-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c5778a1b-fd7c-4eca-bb21-1cc22c72a027/adsu202400296-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,c) The first discharge and the 2<sup>nd</sup> charge are shown as CVs &nbsp; for the materials showing the highest first discharge capacities within the Na = 0.67 system. b,d) The same data shown as voltage curves. Specifically, the compositions are Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.52</sub>Ni<sub>0.24</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (a,b) and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (c,d).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Given the strong performance of the optimal P2 material, we explored the mechanisms behind the electrochemistry more thoroughly with ex-situ XAS and XPS. To further investigate the valence changes of manganese, nickel, and iron, ex situ XAS studies were conducted on our optimized material, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, upon Na ions intercalation/deintercalation. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0008">8a–c</a> shows XANES data for Mn K-edge, Fe K-edge, and Ni K-edge. The oxidation state of the Mn in the pristine state is clearly very close to 4<sup>+</sup>. Upon charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, no change was observed in the oxidation state of the Mn as oxidation beyond Mn<sup>4+</sup> is not possible in this potential window. However, discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V altered the oxidation state of Mn to ≈3.5 and certainly not all the way to 3. The Ni K-edge spectra showed that Ni is synthesized in the 2.5<sup>+</sup> state, then oxidized to Ni<sup>3+</sup> during the charging process and this process is reversible. No distinct change was observed for the Fe K-edge at different charge/discharge states suggesting that iron ions are electrochemically inactive during cycling, it also appears to be in an oxidation state above 3<sup>+</sup> based on the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reference material.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6988538b-8317-4f2e-9d16-8e7add58c8d1/adsu202400296-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6988538b-8317-4f2e-9d16-8e7add58c8d1/adsu202400296-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b5ca3581-e5fb-4f71-96ed-cd53bb78df42/adsu202400296-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6988538b-8317-4f2e-9d16-8e7add58c8d1/adsu202400296-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XANES spectra at the a) Mn K-edge, b) Fe K-edge, and c) Ni K-edge of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material collected at pristine (2.7&nbsp;V), charged (4.3&nbsp;V), and discharged (1.5&nbsp;V) states. d–f) The corresponding pair distribution functions extracted from the EXAFS spectra.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We further explore whether the observed changes in XANES spectra account for the full specific capacity observed electrochemically using the same approach as used in ref. [<span><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0027_R_d53838689e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Mater.">27</a></span>] First, the reversible Ni oxidation during charge results in an edge shift of 1.5&nbsp;eV in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0008">8a</a> which corresponds to an oxidation state change of 1.0 in comparison to the edge shift in the reference materials. Based on the stoichiometry of the material, this gives 42 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> due to nickel redox during both charge and discharge. The actual charge capacity was 135 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> such that transition metal redox does not account for 93 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and we attribute this to anionic redox. During discharge, Mn is also reduced with an edge shift of 2.47&nbsp;eV which corresponds to a change in oxidation state of 0.54 by comparing to the reference materials which shift by 4.5&nbsp;eV. This change in oxidation state of Mn accounts for 84 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> such that Ni/Mn redox together account for 126 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Since the full discharge was 235 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> this means that 109 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be attributed to anionic redox during discharge. We consider the values during charge and discharge (109 and 93 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) to be well within the precision of this technique and thus demonstrate the reversibility of the anionic redox in this material. Thus, within the explored 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V potential window, the measured capacity for the Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> results from the redox of Ni, Mn, and O.</p>
            
            <p>The evolution of the Fourier-transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) spectra in different charge–discharge states is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0008">8d–f</a>. Two intense peaks were observed for Mn, Fe, and Ni K-edges, corresponding to the average length of the M─O bond of the first shell and the average length of the M─M bond of the second shell, respectively. As Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0008">8d</a> shows, Mn─O bond lengths do not change significantly during the charge process, but it does increase slightly during discharge to 1.5&nbsp;V as Mn<sup>4+</sup> is reduced to the larger Mn<sup>3+</sup> during the deintercalation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0008">8f</a> shows that the Ni─O bond length undergoes a reversible decrease/increase upon charge/discharge, consistent with the Ni ions being electrochemically active and compensating for the charge balance during the deintercalation of sodium from the crystal lattice which is in good agreement with the XANES results. For the second shells, all the M─M interatomic distances are increasing by oxidation of transition metals and decreasing by their reduction. Finally, for Fe–O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0008">8e</a>), we see a decrease in the Fe─O bond length during discharge to 1.5&nbsp;V compared to the pristine at 2.7&nbsp;V. We attribute this to distortions to the Fe─O bond due to the enlarged Mn ions in the neighboring shells. There is also a significant increase in the symmetry of this peak in the fully charged material compared to both the pristine and discharged samples (this even shows a small new peak in the PDF). This shows significant ordering of the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra during charge, and then restructuring during discharge.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, to contrast the behavior of our optimized material, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, in the bulk and surface, XPS measurements were performed upon Na ions intercalation/deintercalation. Results are summarized in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0009">9</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The nickel XPS peaks show oxidation of nickel during charge with no corresponding reduction during discharge, with the average nickel oxidation states being 2.47 (pristine), 2.71 (charged), and 2.80 (discharged). Consistent with our previous study in a Ni containing Li layered oxide,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0027_R_d53838689e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> there is some systematic reduction of nickel at the surface of the charged material compared to the bulk as seen by XAS (3<sup>+</sup> according to XANES). Evaluating the splitting between the two Mn 3S peaks is an accurate method to determine the Mn oxidation state. As Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0009">9d,e</a> shows, pristine and charged samples show a ≈4.5&nbsp;eV splitting, consistent with Mn<sup>4+</sup>. For the discharged sample, splitting changed to ≈5.2&nbsp;eV suggesting that Mn is reduced to ≈3<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0028_R_d53838689e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Phys.: Condens. Matter.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe was identified at 3<sup>+</sup> state in all the pristine, charged, and discharged samples by the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak position of ≈ 711.1&nbsp;eV with a satellite peak splitting of ≈8.3&nbsp;Ev<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0029_R_d53838689e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Surf. Interface Anal.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting Fe is an electrochemically inactive species which is similar to XANES findings. Overall, it can be concluded that the behavior of the Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material is quite similar on the surface and in the bulk.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adsu202400296-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d80fa4e5-349c-4414-8588-5a063a736e17/adsu202400296-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d80fa4e5-349c-4414-8588-5a063a736e17/adsu202400296-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/db51f67b-9e29-48e3-b715-3072bd77d276/adsu202400296-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d80fa4e5-349c-4414-8588-5a063a736e17/adsu202400296-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adsu202400296-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadsu.202400296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectra of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material collected at the pristine (2.7&nbsp;V), charged (4.3&nbsp;V), and discharged (1.5&nbsp;V) states: a–c) Ni-2p, d–f) Mn-3S, and g–i) Fe-2p<sub>3/2</sub> regions.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We, therefore, conclude that the optimal Na–Fe–Mn–Ni–O material harnessed Ni, Mn, and O redox processes while Fe remained inactive and thereby offered improved stability during cycling.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adsu202400296-sec-0060-title">2.4 Accelerated Aging</h3>
            
            <p>As mentioned above, poor air stability is a limiting factor for commercializing of oxides as Na-ion cathodes. In this study, an accelerated aging protocol was used to investigate air stability. XRD patterns were collected from single P2 phase materials both in the pristine state and after keeping them for one week in humid air (RH = 95%–97%). After exposure to humid air, a second phase, Birnessite, appeared in the XRD patterns for nearly all samples as demonstrated previously in the doped Na–Mn–O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0017_R_d53838689e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The percentage of the P2 phase after exposure to humid air is mapped in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adsu202400296-fig-0003">3i–g</a>. Results showed a trend between air stability and composition for all three Na levels. Increasing the amount of Ni improves air stability, though the most dramatic improvement is found by reducing and eliminating iron. Interestingly, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.68</sub>Ni<sub>0.32</sub>O<sub>2</sub> retained 100% of the pristine P2 phase after the full week of accelerated aging. This indeed shows the importance of tuning the nickel content as discussed previously in relation to the electrochemical performance.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202400296-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>The current study systematically and thoroughly explores the impact of composition on the performance of Na-ion cathodes in the pseudo-quaternary Na–Fe–Mn–Ni–O system. Such thorough explorations of Gibbs’ pyramids are exceedingly rare in the literature and are indeed desperately needed in situations where optimal compositions exist and cannot be determined without such a deep dive into the impact of composition. Here, the solubility of all 4 cations in the commercially relevant P2 phase is systematically determined. It is found that saturating the phase with nickel leads to both optimal battery performance and improved stability in air, both improvements being badly needed to utilize these cathodes in practical devices. Importantly, the negative capacity can also be minimized with nickel saturation, such that a common drawback of the P2 phase that it lowers the average voltage and requires sacrificial Na is no longer true. The optimum P2 material identified here is therefore highly competitive with the best O3 materials reported in the literature. Though further optimization with other dopants is certainly possible and is the subject of future research, the current study clearly shows the importance of systematic screening through highly complex composition spaces in order to meet the stringent demands required to displace state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries with the more sustainable Na-ion technology.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adsu202400296-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0110-title"> Material Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the synthesis in this study was done by applying high throughput methods. Materials were synthesized using a combinatorial sol-gel synthesis as validated in detail in refs. [<span><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0014_R_d53838689e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Eng. Au.">14</a>-<a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0016_R_d53838689e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Can. J. Chem.">16</a></span>]. Specifically, samples were made using 2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> solutions of NaNO<sub>3</sub>, Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Mn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2,</sub> and Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, where all precursors were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich (note: all solutions were tested with ICP prior to use to determine their concentrations given some are hydrates with variable water content). First, varying volumes of each of the above solutions were dispensed according to target stoichiometries into an 8 × 8 array of wells in a standard 96-well plate. A total volume of 45&nbsp;µL was dispensed into each well using precision micropipettes. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) shows an image of such mixtures in a Gibbs’ triangle showing the clear change in composition of the mixtures with the color evolving smoothly over the triangle. Next, the chelating agent, citric acid (3&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>), was added in a 1: 1 molar ratio (citric acid to the total metal cations). Then, samples were heated at 65&nbsp;°C for 2 days to form homogeneous precursor gels. The resulting gels were crushed into powder and transferred on an alumina plate. An 8 × 8 aluminum smokestack was mounted on the samples to provide independent combustion chambers and prevent cross-contamination. Then, samples were heated at 400&nbsp;°C for 2 h at a rate of 2&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> to decompose the nitrates and citric acid. Finally, the smokestack was removed, and samples were heated at 850&nbsp;°C for 12 h at a rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> in a box furnace in the air. The cooling process to the room temperature was done at a rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> too. In total, 256 cathode materials were synthesized by using the above procedure for this study.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0120-title"> X-ray Diffraction</h4>
                  
                  <p>Samples were characterized by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) in high throughput mode (64 scans in 10&nbsp;h). The XRD patterns were collected using a Panalytical Empyrean diffractometer with a Mo source (60&nbsp;kV, 40&nbsp;mA) and GalaPIX area detector in transmission mode. The scattering angle range for Mo Kα radiation (λ = 0.70926 Å for Kα1) was 4–30°, which is approximately equal to the 10–70° angle range for Cu radiation. Each scan was completed in 10 min and resulted in intensities of over 1000 counts for the main peaks which is suitable for further Rietveld refinements. For all XRD patterns shown in this article, the Mo Kα<sub>2</sub> peaks were stripped, and angles were converted to the angles corresponding to Cu-Kα1 (λ = 1.54051 Å) for ease of comparison to the literature, as described in ref. [<span><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0016_R_d53838689e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Can. J. Chem.">16</a></span>]. Overall, 448 XRD measurements were performed for sample characterization including 256 for pristine, and 192 for aged samples (aging method described below). Panalytical's HighScore Plus software was used for the identification of phases and refinement of all XRD patterns.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemistry</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electrochemical tests were performed using combinatorial cells, which allow the cycling of 64 cathode materials at the same time as described in detail in refs. [<span><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0015_R_d53838689e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Comb. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0016_R_d53838689e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Can. J. Chem.">16</a>, <a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0018_R_d53838689e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">18</a></span>]. Briefly, composite cathodes were deposited on aluminum contacts on a custom-designed printed circuit board (PCB, Optima Tech) with 64 parallel channels. The cathode electrodes were made by combining 2&nbsp;mg of active materials and ≈20&nbsp;wt.% carbon black and transferring this mixture onto the pad on the PCB. The electrode loading of the cathode material was ≈20&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Then, a mixture of N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) with polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) was drop-casted onto the samples on the PCB to yield ≈20&nbsp;wt.% PVDF in the final electrode. To vaporize the NMP, the PCB was dried overnight at 50&nbsp;°C. The combinatorial cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The electrolyte used for making the combinatorial cells was 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium perchlorate in propylene carbonate (PC) with 2&nbsp;wt.% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Sodium metal foil and GF/D glass microfiber prefilter were used as the anode, and separator respectively. Electrochemistry in this work was investigated by performing cyclic voltammetry (CV) with the voltage range of 1.5 to 4.3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;V h<sup>−1</sup> on an in-house assembled pseudopotentiostat described in ref. [<span><a href="#adsu202400296-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adsu202400296-bib-0030_R_d53838689e1257" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">30</a></span>]. Average voltages and specific capacities were calculated from the raw data for the multiple cycles. The voltage profiles were also obtained by first integrating current versus time to get the capacity.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0140-title"> Accelerated Aging</h4>
                  
                  <p>To study the air stability of these materials accelerated aging experiments were performed. Single-phase materials (pure P2) for sodium contents of <i>x</i> = 0.55, 0.67, and 0.8 were kept in an enclosed sealed plastic container for one week. The humidity level was measured with a humidity meter and was maintained at 95%–97% using a saturated K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> solution as the vapor source. The temperature was maintained at 23 ± 1&nbsp;°C. Powder X-ray diffraction was performed on all samples after one week of storage in high humidity conditions.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0150-title"> Sodium Loss</h4>
                  
                  <p>To measure the extent of sodium loss during the synthesis, various samples from the phase diagram were chosen for elemental composition analysis using inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES). First, samples were dissolved in aqua regia and then diluted down to measurable concentrations. Measurements were done using an Agilent Technologies 5100 ICP-OES that can run 20 samples per hour in an automated fashion.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0160-title"> X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy</h4>
                  
                  <p>To study the redox processes and local structure environment evolution during the charge/discharge process in the optimized Na–Fe–Mn–Ni–O material, ex situ X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) measurements were performed at the Hard X-ray Micro-Analysis (HXMA) beamline at the Canadian Light Source (CLS). XANES and EXAFS spectra for Mn, Fe, and Ni K-edges were collected in both the transmission and fluorescent modes. Three samples were made: a pristine Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.16</sub>Fe<sub>0.24</sub>O<sub>2</sub> sample (2.7&nbsp;V), the same material after charging to 4.3&nbsp;V and this same material after charging to 4.3&nbsp;V followed by a discharge to 1.5&nbsp;V. For this, Swagelok cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox using Na foil as anode, GF/D glass microfiber prefilter as separator and the same electrolyte as in the combinatorial cells. The cathodes were composed of 20&nbsp;mg active material and ≈20 wt.% carbon black. Galvanostatic cycling was performed at 0.1 V h<sup>−1</sup>. Swagelok cells were then opened in an argon-filled glovebox to retrieve and rinse the cathode material. Three rinsing cycles with dimethyl carbonate (DMC) were done and, finally, the remaining powder was dried overnight in the ambient argon gas in the glovebox. Next, ≈30&nbsp;mg of each sample was sealed between layers of Kapton tape in a glovebox and then transferred to the CLS in sealed pouches.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adsu202400296-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adsu202400296-sec-0170-title"> X-ray Photoemission Spectroscopy (XPS)</h4>
                  
                  <p>To study the near-surface oxidation states, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were performed on the same samples studied with XAS. Measurements were performed on a Thermo Fisher-Scientific Nexsa G2 XPS using a monochromatic Al K(alpha) source (15&nbsp;mA, 15&nbsp;kV). Peak fitting was performed using the Advantage software.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
